RATING: 8
One of the best combinations of theme and mechanics that I've had the pleasure to play, and probably one of the best adaptations of a book that I've ever seen. It brings to the table all the main elements that make the universe of Dune such a great scenario: the struggling factions, the endless intrigues and, of course, the unique essence of the planet Arrakis. Everything is reflected by the rules that, even if not always perfect, manage in offering to the players a simple system with a good level of strategy and just a bit of luck. It can be really long and it's difficult to estimate the real duration of a game. Depending on how the various alliances develop, it can be a matter of a couple of hours or it can go on for 4-5 hours. Is it worth the time investment? More than in other cases, it’s definitely a matter of personal taste. Nowadays there are more accessible alternatives for the same category of games, but what really makes Dune stand out is its unique blend of theme and rules. One thing is for sure: you'll spend that whole time negotiating, debating and considering your military opportunities, in a deeply interactive game that doesn't leave much space to downtime. Of course, if you  don’t know the universe depicted by the book the game probably loses at least one point or two, but, despite its age, it’s still a good piece of work.

Avalon Hill version, components of good quality and design (considering their age). It’s worth to mention that here on BGG there is some great homemade material that is definitely worth a look.

RATING: 4
Dune is basically just an inferior Cosmic Encounter knock off with some really good ideas.  It's inferior because the added complexity distracts from the player interaction. You spend more time fiddling with rules than playing your opponents.

I love the process of bidding for cards--only some players know what everyone is actually bidding for. The combat resolution, including possible traitors, is fantastic. Someone should steal this idea. Bizarrely and unforgivably the combat resolution was modified when FFG publish its Dune knock-off Rex.

Superficially it captures the feeling of the book it's based on. This feeling goes away after a few plays when the CE internals start to manifest in a gamey and un-Dune like fashion. In the end the great ideas are wasted on a game that is too much limited by the source material and its similarity to CE. If you want Dune, read the book. If you want a good game of shifting alliances, play CE. Dune the boardgame fuctions best as a novelty collectible.

RATING: 7
This game is a ton of fun, but tends to be over very quickly for our group. The first time the sandworm appears, we hold a conference, everyone creates alliances, and the game is pretty much over three turns after that. Alliances are very powerful. I have been on the winning side once, and the losing side once – the first time I played Harkonnen and was the back-stabbiest of players – I also chose my traitors very well. We were able to wipe out the opposition because I had all their major generals as traitors – which was great for us, terrible for them. 

The second game was much closer – in that I played the Guild and was allied with the Emperor. And even though we lost, I was able to nuke an entire city – that alone made the game worth playing. I lost one troop (the guys who set up the nuke), was able to take out twenty enemies troops, and one city was no longer protected from the sandstorm. It was glorious. I will probably never have the chance to nuke the shield wall again... but I will never forget the time I did!

RATING: 7
Very chaotic and unsettling at first, especially with game end conditions that could end the game at any time in an unpredictable way. In fact, our alliance won in the 3 or 4 of a possible 15 rounds. 

I do really like the storm mechanic that destroy everything in it's path (soft reset of the game winning area control spots). And I really like the battle mechanic made of of base units in area (you pick how many you want to use, but make sure to leave at least 1 behind so that you can occupy the space if you win), the power of your leader, and 1 defense and 1 attack treachery card. 

2 hours to go over the rules for first-time players in a 5 player game. Not sure how long the game actually went. I was the mind-controlling, free-deployment-to-planet Bene Gesserit witches. Out of all of Silverbeard's out-of-print games, this is a fantastic find!

BGG reviews: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/303598/first-time-reviewer-for-dune, http://www.boardgamegeek.com/thread/273666/dune-the-perfect-blend-of-euro-and-at

RATING: 6
This is essentially a war game with each player having certain assymetrical powers.  The game uses mechanics and game play to recreate the feeling of an undoubtedly beloved work of science fiction.  Since I have not read Dune, when I played Dune, I was essentially left with experiencing just the game itself, not the sensation of reliving my experience of reading Dune.  As such, I found the game itself to be merely an okay war game, not something I would want to play again, so I have to give it a 6 at best.  

The blind bidding for Treachery cards which are fairly decisive in battles, with one player being able to see what they are, was a weird way to effectively and eventually determine battle results.  I did not care for this mechanic, but it is an interesting game design idea.  Bottom line: if one wanted to play a war game, or a war game with alliances, there are better games.  If one wanted to play a game with assymetric player powers, alliances, and zinging card effect battles, I would go with Cosmic Encounter.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dune does a lot of things right: its extreme asymmetry manages to seem moderately balanced; its combat system is elegant and quick; and in particular, its diplomacy and shared victory conditions smooth out most balance issues and add variability between games. 

However, the game has a significant snowball effect: once you start losing combats, it seems difficult to recover, due to the disproportionate advantages for winning. The winner gets to keep his hero, most treachery cards, and (most egregiously) gets paid for any enemy heroes they kill. The loser kind of gets hosed: not only do they lose their troops, but also treachery cards, which are key to winning combats. The loser tends to be both low on dough and treachery cards at this point, which makes it difficult to win further combats.

Traitor cards are meant to add another variable to perhaps even things out (as well as be conceptually one of the coolest things about the game) but in practice the odds of seeing a relevant traitor was so low as to not bear thinking about. 

Would love to try playing again with a few house rules. Note: we played with a print-and-play version of the game; good luck finding an original copy.

This review might be partially influenced by me getting crushed as fremen, so take it with a grain of spice.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Wow!
Dune has it all. Bluffing. Risk. Alliances, Traitors. Sacrifice. Leadership. Bidding.
Few games ever made have had as many positive attributes as Dune has. Sure, by today&#039;s standards it is overly long and some of the rules (BG co-existence) are laborious, but once players have mastered the rules, the game has more dimension and tension than almost anything on the market today.
Key to play are bidding, bluffing and collection. Players bid for cards that allow them to perform various functions in the game or, more importantly, triumph in battles. You bid to risk your wealth acquiring the tools to triumph...or not!
Conflict resolution is a bluffing system unlike any other game. Leaders make the difference but each player secretly allocates how many precious troops they are willing to sacrifice. Too few and they lose the battle and all is lost anyway. Too many and the victory is Pyrhhic in that other factions will exploit your depleted position. Leadership most often makes the difference in battles, but beware traitors.
The economics of the game revolve around the collection of spice from the surface. Too little collection and everyone is poor and desperate. Too much and some factions (Emperor, Harkonnen) become overly powerful.
Dune is a game of balance and subtgerfuge.
The theme is so much in line with the books that you really take on the mechanics of a faction as if you were the fictional characters.
The real standout of the design is the assymetrical structure of the factions. Some factions are far better than others, but often it depends when in the game: beginning, middle or end. The ebb and flow of power really is like sand dunes shifting. Sweet victories come from sacrifice and risk, but mostly from just simple outmaneuvering and outthinking your opponents.
I don&#039;t reccomend the Spice Harvest expansion. The Duel expansion is better but the one-on-one fighting is boring. You can opt out of that portion and simply use the leaders and new cards/rules to enhance the basic game.
Last point: No game in this database deserves a remake like this one.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Great game. Captures Herbert's Dune exquisitly. The components serve to immerse one in the world of Dune - the Battle Wheeels are an inspired notion ("Wheels within wheels"). The game has it all: player characters, each with their own powers; combat which is simple to grasp, but excruciating in its choices; diplomacy, bluffing, treachery and betrayal; Resources (spice and troops) which must be acquired and managed efficiently; leaders, which are vitally important for combat; a bidding mechanism in which spice is bid on cards that convey special abilities; victory which is based on area control. I preferred a house rule which disallowed Alliance wins.

RATING: 7
I've only played this game once, so my ratings may go up for this, but I was heavily impressed with the level of diplomacy and position-shifting involved in this game.  Alliances shift often, so you may be on the verge of victory one turn, and then all of a sudden, in a "loser" alliance the next turn.  Powers keep it interesting - particularly trying to gage when and who the Bene Gesserit want to win.  It does appear that the powers are slightly unbalanced (with the Fremen appearing to be weakest).

I played the BG's the first and only time, so would be interested in playing again with a different race.

RATING: 4
Played twice, both times as a 6-player basic game using the optional rules: XV, XVI, XVII & XIX.  We played without using any expansions.

The strength and weakness of Dune is its alliance system.  Alliances may only be formed or broken when a Worm card (aka nexus) appears, which is randomly determined by the spice deck.  Ideally a nexus will appear every few turns so that the alliance system is dynamic and fluid.  However (much like the Westeros deck in A Game of Thrones) the deck shuffle can break the game.  Unfortunately, the spice deck distribution ruined both of my sessions.  
 
During my first session, a Worm did not appear until Turn 9, which is a really long time to wait!  The second time I played, a Worm appeared Turn 6, which would have been OK, but when a Worm does appear, you keep drawing until you draw a Territory card.  Unfortunately, the next 4 cards were Worms, leaving only 1 Worm card for the next 9 Turns (FYI- It finally appeared Turn 14.)  
    
So, does the alliance system in Dune make for a good game?  Well, in theory it sounds pretty cool.  Each faction has a variable set of player powers that they bring to the table.  These advantages can be combined together in some interesting ways.  However, the alliance system sort of sucks.  As stated, alliances are at the mercy of the spice deck, which can easily break the game. The other thing that sucks is that the game encourages alliances so much that you are almost forced into a counter-alliance if one or more factions join together.  I guess you can be sneaky and get a solo victory, but in most cases you have to share the win with one or more players, which SUCKS.      

I MIGHT play Dune again for I highly enjoy exploiting the variable player powers & traitors.  I also enjoy the secret selection of Leaders, Cards, & Troops during battle.  Perhaps with some house rules to fix the alliance system, I will be more inclined to play again... but why bother trying to fix Dune when I can simply play a better multi-player war game?

RATING: 5

Rating dropped accordingly.

RATING: 6
Although I have to acknowledge it’s a good game, I really can’t seem to enjoy Dune: actually it isn’t anything else than a purely mathematical game where you only have to calculate your odds again and again before (and after) taking any action.

Moreover, its theme (Herbert’s saga and setting) has always left me aloof.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Dune is a clear case of nostalgia and sentiment trumping critical appraisal.  I would even argue that Dune fails to achieve the accurate and thick theme many apprise in it.  After all, when did the various factions ever come engage in full-scale war as depicted in this game?

Speaking of war, this is where the game truly crumbles as it fails to capture any strategic dynamic or tactical finesse.  Since only the strongholds matter and perhaps a couple spaces on the board with spice, most of the board rarely comes into play.  No exciting maneuvers, no logistical consequences and no tactical considerations.  Just ship into a (cheaper) stronghold and send a few guys to collect some nearby spice.  This wouldn't be a completely terrible model if not for the suicidal combat resolution.  All your guys die if you are vanquished.  If you win, even with one guy left, you control the stronghold and you keep your treachery cards, along with a hefty bonus of spice for killed leaders.  So most battles you dial one less than your maximum force pool, except that most leaders contribute about as much to victory so actual force count is irrelevant, then again a completely random weapon and armor system makes leaders very fragile.  Synthesizing all these elements, you have a system with such a huge discrepancy between winner and loser that it encourages Pyrrhic victories and wild swings of fate endlessly repeated as players reinforce directly into strongholds.

On top of this is a ludicrous economic model that fails to function in either the basic game or the advanced game and is subject to very strange perturbations based on which powers are in play.

Finally, we come to the novel alliance system, which unfortunately loses much of its appeal with the shallow nature of the rest of the game.  Alliances function mostly to leverage certain power combinations (like Atreides looking at treachery cards and the Imperium buying them for free) and meet the victory conditions rather than registering any strategic impact.  Unsatisfying to say the least.

Yet, for all of this it does maintain some charm imparted to it by the rich setting of Herbert's creation.  Even that, however, can't save a game that runs too long and rarely offers up any interesting decisions.

RATING: 10
I saw a description of the David Lynch's "Dune" movie entitled, "Still crazy after all these years." This encapsulates the experience of the board game perfectly as well. It can run very long. There are rules questions that pop up that veteran Dune groups have answered differently in good faith. Each group has to decide which set of rules with which to play. There is a definite learning curve. It's out of print. ...and it's awesome. I owe my thanks to Slev for the rules rewrite, Ilya for the graphic redesign and Tullsen for the production; I have and can play this classic now. 

The theme is rich (I've played Rex and this matters more than I had thought). The asymmetry reveals itself in the blend of alliances, producing moments of just dazzling surprise in each session. The battles, particularly because of the traitor element, frequently involve the players holding their breath for the outcome to be revealed. My main group doesn't really have the patience for this but the game is worth finding a group that does. If I had one BGG wish to be granted, I'd wish for the Herbert Estate to grant Fantasy Flight the rights to produce the game with Iya's redesign. I won't hold my breath, but I will relish my opportunity to play this title. Get it on Ebay. Print and play it. Just play it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A great game that has not lost its charm with the years. The players struggle to control a set number of strongholds on the planet while harvesting "spice" which provides income necessary to keep one's forces strong. The key elements from Herbert's classic books are skilfully woven together to form perhaps the best matching of theme to game ever seen. The "battle wheel" approach to combat is unique and fiendishly clever.

This game is best played with at least five or six, as strange things can happen with fewer, and choosing which factions to leave out is not a simple matter. More players also bring out the interesting alliance possibilities that can have drastic effects on the game's duration and outcome. Games will last anywhere from three turns to fifteen depending on the group dynamics (I highly recommend using the "almost official" rules regarding the number of strongholds needed for alliance victories). The designers from Eon who did this and "Cosmic Encounter" set the scene for what we call Euros today.

RATING: 9
Oh, what a blast that game was.  Let me make it clear that I find no boardgame experience to be more fulfilling than successfully defeating 5 other players in a battle of wits, diplomacy, and war.  Thus, you will find that my top 3 games require these skills in spades.  However, no other game can provide one with the feeling of ultimate devious pleasure than winning a game of Dune as the Bene Gesserit through their prescience ability!

I was fortunate in my first and only game to be randomly assigned the faction I was looking most forward to playing (Bene Gesserit).  I successfully foretold that the Fremen would win in Turn 7.  Well, it was more than luck as I worked subtly so that they would succeed (e.g. weakening a city here or there to make it open for the Fremen to take).  It required me to push against the Fremen too (for on Turn 6 they might have won had I not attacked them).  

Unfortunately, I had it in the bag until one person (Mr. Bourn the Harkonnen, of course) recalled, after the game was decided, that the final combat between Fremen and Corrino's was a tie and not a win for the Fremen (that would have given me the game due to my prescience).  Thus the Fremen did not capture the last city on T7 as I had predicted.  Bahh, a mere technicality I say!

RATING: 9
-sold-

sleeved

Avalon Hill "Sting"

I often heard how well Dune implements its mechanics into the theme. Well that really is true.
For me, as a fan of Dune (books, movies and games), this game transports the story and the feeling of the Dune-universe incredibly well.
Every faction feels like it is represented in the movies.

What I really like, besides the well balanced, asymmetric factions, is the alliance-mechanism. Most games don't feature true alliances. But here it offers more than empty promises and eventually backstabbing, no, here it gives you benefits if you work together.
Absolutely great game and another proof that old games have a lot to offer.

RATING: 9
I used to play this a *lot* 15 years ago, and then might have rated it a 9 or 10. It's fallen off a lot recently, though, with a lot of the longer games that no longer seem such good bang for the time investment anymore. Dune really is much too long for today, and I am unlikely to play again despite the wonderful ambiance, amongst the best game adaptations of a book/movie ... by a wide margin. I'll break with the conventional wisdom, I think 5 is best ... unless you can find someone who explicitly *wants* to play the Bene Gesserit. They can be quite powerful, but they also can be really, really boring as they take forever to build up strength and have a horrible time getting money. The Guild may have sucky leaders, but at least they're usually flush. Anyway, as interesting as the BG can be, I wouldn't want to inflict them on anyone who wasn't enthused about playing them. The Tleilaxu expansion for the General is facinating, an interesting alternate 6th (or even 7th) power, and hardly *less* involved than the BG. For a good bloodbath to help out the Tleilaxu, play with The Duel. Decidedly *not* a fan of the Landsraad as their "hoser" power seems to be against the spirit of the game.

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play: Just played this for the first time, and thought it was great (I won, as Emperor!). Since four people were learning though, we didn't use the alliance rules. I look forward to playing again using them. This game is remarkably well-themed. It evokes the feel of the books near-perfectly. All the powers are incredibly well-balanced; in fact, I think its only fault is that I really wouldn't want to play without max players... if any one of the powers were missing, I think things would feel a bit off.

Update: played again 6 years later in a comical game where my boyfriend won on TURN ONE. So, didn't get much time to re-assess my feelings on the game, but a few things jumped out at me:

-Coming to this game some 20+ years after its release is sort of harsh, just in that the presentation is so poor. There's a LOT to take in for each race, and it's sort of spread out between your individual character sheet, generic reference sheet, and the rulebook. A component update would make this game 100x more learnable. It's a shame it will probably never be re-released.

-Remembered a couple of things that feel like they could be streamlined. Notably, the whole half-point system for committing units feels just plain convoluted.

There's probably a few other rough edges that could be worked out, but I didn't get the chance to experience them! I remember my first game took somewhere in the neighborhood of 2 hours, which I felt was unnecessarily long (making game 2 all the funnier, I guess). Hopefully I can get a better feel for the game by playing again... sometime before 6 years from now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The classic assymetrical game and one of the cleverest translations of theme from book to game. The different faction abilities and possibility of traitors are true to the book and make for a tense game where you take on a role rather than a colour. Should be played with the additional character advantages (not to be mistaken for the advanced rules which are more hassle than they're worth). Game length seems to be highly variable. The mechanics were groundbreaking when it first came out but might feel rather 'clunky' to modern eurogamers. The auctions can slow the game down if not everyone is paying attention. I would like to try having the player whose turn it is start the bidding and then open it up after that.

This game really deserves to be reprinted with updated components (better cards, shields and tokens, bigger board and more concise rules). Some lovely resources available in the files section to improve the look of the game thanks to several creative geeks. The Herbert estate's refusal to re-licence this game is odd from an accountant's viewpoint and a huge disappointment for fans of the book and game.

Previously owned two copies of the AH edition and the Descartes French edition. Now own a P&P version cobbled together from the best bits of five different editions / re-designs! Includes stickered wooden tokens and three decks of ArtsCow cards.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Rated 4 after one play. This is a tough game to rate, as there's a lot to really appreciate with this game. For a game released in 1979, there's a surprising range of fun and innovative mechanics. The auction for "treachery cards" is actually pretty solid, and possibly the best aspect of the game is the unique roles of each player. 

We played with the full complement of six, and it was fun to watch the interactions between roles that encourage combat, boost money, provide hidden information, etc. These roles aren't minor tweaks; they are serious power differences that enrich the gameplay.

The Achilles heel is the play time and the slow move to an endgame. In three hours, we had two or three moments when either a player or an alliance between two players came close to ending it, only to watch the rest of the players rebalance and kick 'em out of position. It's a slow crawl to finish the game, and given the lengthy playtime, I'd rather be playing some modern options that have a better game arc. Still, anyone curiously interested in some of the "grandfathers" of modern gaming need to play this at least once.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
This is my favorite game. The Bene Gesserit rules really make for some interesting play. They predict who will win and when and write this down before the game begins. If they successfully predict the outcome, they win. So imagine an alliance round, do you ally with the Bene Gesserit-- they might be trying to really help you, or they could throw the fight so the other side could win, making them win. Everything they say has multiple meanings...

RATING: 9
Probably this is THE negotiation game I'm looking for. The premise of negotiation game is you need to have possibility to need help or helping other players, and so mechanically your move should be affected a lot by other player actions. It's pretty easy to fall on each end (Full diplomacy like Cosmic Encounter or, well, Diplomacy, or maybe full mechanical like Chicago Express or Container). But Dune strikes the perfect balance of mechanic and diplomacy. 

Not to mention the special abilities of each faction is clearly bringing out the Dune theme a lot. Theme is dripping! The only downside is it's truly at its best at 6 player. Less is probably playable, but I don't know.

RATING: 7
A game the alleged merit of which has taken on mythological qualities, due partly to a very vocal number of people one can count on one's hand, and due partly to its relative inaccessibility.  If this game were ever to be republished, I'm convinced the overall impression would be "OK, that's cool, but it's not THAT cool."It is a system that can lead players to fritter away four to six hours, only to watch as two players - in desperation for the damn thing to end - form an unholy alliance that completely unbalances the forces at odds, and pulls the entire rug out of under everyone.  Clever ideas?  Yes, some, but certainly not the end-all or be-all for implementation thereof.

The ultimate irony for it, perhaps, is that it is a standard bearer for the Ameri-Trash crowd, yet by far best typifies some of the nature of Euro-games, well before they were pigeonholed as such - no dice for combat, hidden bidding, resource-collection, etc.

RATING: 7
A must-play for any hardcore Dune fan like myself and probably not the best choice for someone who isn't familiar with the story as the interplay and special rules are a bit more intuitive if you already understand the factions (unlike say Game of Thrones where not having read the books will not prevent you from understanding or enjoying the game)

The game was long, it was intense, at times even frustrating.  I preferred it once the alliances came into play, which in hour game wasn't for several hours.  There really is some opportunity for brilliant maneuvers here, the kind that get you so excited when you manage to pull off.

This is a game I probably wouldn't play more than once a year because of the length and intensity, but I would be unhappy to go more than a year or two without playing it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A lot of the older games are propped up with nostalgia: they really don't hold up that well against newer offerings.  AH games, I've found, often find themselves in this category.  Dune does not.  Dune is something special.  It marries a perfectly implemented and rich theme, with excellent mechanics.  Yes the rules can be fiddly, but they are so for a purpose.  If you're a fan of the books, you will feel very comfortable in a game of Dune.  Even if you're not familiar, you'll find Dune is probably the best multiplayer wargame ever made.  It will be tough sledding to learn the differences between the factions, but you'll have plenty of help because there is some cooperation in this game.  Your strategic, tactical, and diplomatic skills will be pushed to their limits.  The movement in Dune is so spare as to evoke Chess or Go.  Feints and strikes abound.  That said, you'll be amazed at how epic Dune feels despite a maximum of 30 moves.

RATING: 9
(my copy is a print-n-play mounted version constructed from internet downloads)

When playing with 2, be very careful about what factions you use, or else be ready to modify the victory conditions.   Since this game isn't really about balance, it can end REAL fast with two players, but really it's best with 4-6 players anyway and shouldn't be played with just 2.

This is perhaps the pinnacle of the marriage of theme and mechanics.  People who have never read the books or even seen the movie(s) will likely have a hard time caring about this game.   However the design is richly thematic and it's amazing how far ahead of its time all the EON games are. This game has a tendancy to end very quickly, or go the full distance, and not much inbetween. Hummmmmm... Shai-Hulud!!

Based on one play:

I found this game to be the 'heaviest' game I've played yet. It was fun and very challenging. I would definitely play it again...but I don't know when or how often I'm going to have to set aside 4-6 hours to play a single game, and I think I would rather play two games of Cosmic Encounter or get two games in over one game most of the time. I enjoyed the battle mechanics, the storm seemingly always barreling down on you and all the things that go into each move that make this a deep strategic game.

Played the Dune setup expansion once and felt like it was a good time by itself, but not really needed for the base Dune game. RATING: 9.75
I own the french version from Descartes Editeur.

Dune is one of my favorite games. It is another classic board game for me. I always look forward to playing Dune again. I really want to play it again sometime.

The game really shines with 6 players. Playable with 4-5 players but no less if you want a worthwhile game experience. It is better with 6 players since all families are represented.

I would want to play this game with my regular gamers at home, though it can be a long game. 

In 2008 at the WBC, I watched part of the final and it took nearly 9 hours. Reason why it doesn't hit the table today. The more players you have the longer it can take.

UPDATE: I played two games of Dune recently with four players and it was more fun than I first of thought it would be since not all the families are represented. The two games played were played in the same day too. A four player game is a lot shorter than a six player game.

Alliances can be short or long depending how often the worms comes out and if you want to stay in the same alliances or switch to someone else or go out on your own again.

Can't wait to play Dune again.

5 plays since recording games played.

RATING: 9
[5 plays] This is the rare game based on a book that succeeds both in capturing the flavor of the book and in being as good in its own (game) way as a great book.  Read the book first or the game will make no sense whatever. Dune is a magnificent epic game, the kind that generates moments that you remember and talk about long afterward.  Some see its highly variable and potentially long playing time as a big drawback; it can easily go 8 hours, but a victory in the early rounds may wrap it up within a couple of hours.  Of course the long time causes some scheduling issues, but the game is well worth it, and I don't see it as negative in itself; Dune may last for 8 hours, but they'll be 8 really fun and exciting hours.

RATING: 7
[b][thread=512219]The PLAYMOBIL® Legion reviews "Dune"[/thread][/b]

(As of [b]4[/b] plays) After you come to terms with the wildly different special abilities of each faction, actual gameplay is pretty simple. The theme is beautifully wedded to the mechanics. Negotiation, alliances and betrayals add a wonderful layer of interpersonal complexity on top. Only downside is the potential for ludicrously, frequently prohibitively long playing times and, consequently, incomplete games.

[b]2 year verdict:[/b] I really dislike the long playing time, but I wouldn't mind playing again, if the stars and my mood aligned. A truly great negotiation game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Enjoyable, but feels a tad overrated to me.  I do enjoy games with alliances, but they tend to need experienced, or at least cutthroat, players to really shine.  My one play was certainly affected by some negative factors, and I will have to revisit this rating.

Only one of the six had read the book, and it took quite some time to get this creaky antique off the ground (those rules!).  I had the same critique as many others in that most of the board wasn't used in the game.  There seems to be a common 'push-pull' flow in Eon games, where high attrition in combat and fixed victory locations cause forces to go back and forth, repeating moves and fighting battles over and over.  The actual combat is nicely odd, but a bit of a frustratingly obtuse guessing game.

The asymmetrical powers and negotiations and alliances are well done and certainly appear to give the game longevity.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
I’m fortunate to have a group who plays this somewhat regularly (few times a year) and now that we all know the game well enough, the sessions are epic. I’ve had epic victories and epic wipeouts. I’ve been insignificant only to hook up with the winning player at the right time. I’ve been betrayed by my own partner (Bene Gesserit wanted to preserve his prediction so he threw one of our battles!). I’ve been riding high only to lose most of my forces and get kicked to the curb by my partner who goes on to win with someone else. And they’re all epically fun sessions.

This game delivers on what so many others wish they could – a sandbox where players rise and fall in power and alliances constantly shift yet nobody is ever truly out of it. Try getting wiped out in Civ and climbing back in; not likely. The different faction powers and cards mean that a new plan is not too far out of reach.

The space for creative play is wonderfully open. We play with optional faction and Karama powers and you can make some crazy things happen like stealing cards out of a players hand or negating somebody’s special power at the worst time. The asymmetrical faction powers work in a way that makes them feel roughly balanced. Some factions are on WBC record as having won more but they’re all close enough.

I especially like how the alliances work. There’s no fear of Diplomacy or Game of Thrones style out-of-the-blue backstabbing here. You can only ally/un-ally during certain points in the game. I’m so tired of traitor and lying games, and I appreciate the opportunity here to focus on creative power moves vs treachery to a partner. I’m very happy my group has decided instead of trying other games similar to this one to simply play more Dune.

RATING: 7.5
An obvious classic, one of the most thematic games I have played without a doubt and a great recreation of the book.

The battles are bloody and the opportunism rampant.  This wouldn't be so much of a problem if there weren't the constantly shifting alliances which can alter the whole game balance in a turn and create an easy win for the opportunists.  

So, although alliances lie at the heart of this game, they can imbalance the game to make it too lopsided, which is why this keeps my rating at 7.5.

RATING: 10

RATING: 7
a.k.a. "As the Worm Turns" due to the game-altering Alliances enabled by the appearance of the sandworm, Shai-Hulud. Dune is the precursor of the modern Euro-wargame, playing to conclusion in 2-3 hours due to Alliances. Thoroughly entertaining, with the various powers of the factions causing all sorts of havoc (especially with Alliances in force). Never play with less than six, and never sub in the three expansion factions (they ruin the fine balance of the game). The Duel and Spice Harvest expansions are *not* recommended. Otherwise, this is a great game that remains very effective 25 years after it was first published. If you're a fan of the Herbert novels, that only increases the appeal (the flavor of the game setting and mechanics is off the scale).

For a game which is now pushing 30 (it still has a few years), and a title I didn't have the pleasure of playing till well after a multitude of newer designs, I am amazed how this game holds up.  Although games of Dune are hard to organize, as it does require the full compliment of six, when the stars are aligned this is quite simply the most fun I have had playing a game - and might possibly be the closest to being a perfect gaming experience. The theme is rich, the variable powers are designed immaculately, the asymmetry is beautiful especially when alliances are formed and played with treachery.  One can not help but love parading into a Harkonnen strong hold with one Fremen, a lazegun, and a projectile shield.  Dune is a game that leaves you with stories - the kind you never tire of telling or hearing.

UPDATE:  Dune is now more than three decades old, and still going strong.  Only drawback is it needs 6 players to really work and possibly a good chunk of time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
It may have been good for the time it was published, but it has not aged well. Long, boring and incredibly random for a game without dice!

First, you have a series of pointless card auctions where you don't know what you are buying (OK, the Atreides player can see the cards, but it would save a lot of time to allow players just to buy cards at a fixed price and let the Atreides draw two and keep one, for example).

Then, most of the cards are used in combat to kill or defend leaders in a guessing way, with some blind bidding of troops occurring at the same time. The winner may have huge benefits and the loser is severely penalized. Just one or two losses may result in impossible recovery, making the player suffer through the rest of the game - and the playing time may vary immensely. Some cards can also cause huge swings of luck outside of combat, not to mention an absolutely ridiculous special power that allows the Bene Gesserit player to simply steal the victory by guessing the winner and the turn the game ends.

The fascinating universe of Frank Herbert's books and the rich implementation of the theme draw interest to the game, but do little to save it after the novelty goes off (after a few turns). And let's not even talk about production values.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Hard to rate. It surely provides a compelling play experience (even for those like myself with no affinity for Herbertisms--I routinely call the Kwisatz Haderach the Knick-Knack Paddywhack), but the rules are a bit wonky and the game is vastly too long. The movement on the board, in general, is dull and fiddly--especially since most of the action happens via transportation anyway.
All that said, it can produce moments verging on pure brilliance--the daring gambit, the unexpected leader death, the sublime joy at being opposed by a traitor. The first (and only) game I played had a Bene Gesserit win, which was like poetry in its beauty.
I don't think I'll play it again, though, which is a shame. Long past time this got streamlined, and, I for one don't agree that the thematic integration is 100% necessary. Ditch the storm, change the map, keep the combat and traitors... It seems doable.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Holy cow this is a great game.  Played one abbreviated game (we finished, though we ganged up on some people in an alliance to win early because it was dinner time) and despite the abbreviated play had to immediately consider whether or not that was the most fun I had ever had playing a board game.  

Dune captures the "we are unsure who is on our side" tension of battlestar galactica, combines it with an elegant combat mechanism reminiscent of fine area majority war-euro style game, a complex but simple economy of resources like you would expect to see in a great economic game, and has the alliances and backstabbing through a structured environment of Empires in Arms.  Notice I said EiA there and not diplomacy...  backstabbing for backstabbing's own purpose doesn't really work in Dune.

This is a fantastic game everyone should play if they get a chance.  The only problem with it is that the rules, although simple, are not well written at all.  This issue can be alleviated by reading up online, getting some of the clarifications downloaded, and using player aids.  It really is worth the effort.

RATING: 5
Simple rules simulating a complex fictional setting. Dune does a good job recreating some of the themes of the novel and is pretty easy to learn while having some fairly deep strategy. Shai Hulud can wipe out spice production and the coriolis storms can wipe out your units. Interesting interactions can occur when your factions' special abilities work against another's. There's probably a few modern boardgame designers that drew inspiration from this game. If you love the Dune novels (I have for many years) you need to play this game a couple of times. If you like games that give a full strategy boardgame experience you should try it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
An exquisitely interesting, exciting and strategically deep game. I just love how each character is so different than the next but everything hangs together in dynamic, balanced, suspenseful and rhythmic gameplay. Since each player plays a force that has significantly different strengths and weaknesses, each person has a unique role in the game. This makes for a game that shifts and changes in quite a beautiful way, and that would seem to support almost boundless replayability. I really like how the game sets the conflict in a context of a natural disasters like a deadly approaching storm and dangerous sandworms. That really heightens the epic feel.

The person who taught me this game also shared this bit of wisdom: alliances should only be acts of desperation; playing in the spirit of the game means trying to be the sole winner and not settling for a win by alliance.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
Played two 6-player games, both with the Fremen, and was utterly unimpressed.

I had never seen a more unbalanced game, where some players are spectators more than anything else (especially the Freman).

This is a game where the stong will go out of their way to take out the weak, because the winner gets rewarded in spice (the game's currency) for taking out leaders, theirs included.  Unfortunately, that makes the weak a prime target, and they only get weaker, not to say taken out of the "game", if it can be called that.

By pure luck (4 worm signs out of 5 cards), and multiple alliance changes in a row, the Fremen are now somehow in a position to win a battle...  Unfortunately, they don't have any leaders.

In my two games, my impression was that I had no control whatsoever.  It is a diplomatic game more than anything else, but since the Fremen have nothing of value to offer, it is very difficult to enter into an alliance, let alone collect any spice which may help them get out of this predicament.  Does this sound like a solid "10" game?

To add insult to injury, the Atreides sells info to the B.G., letting that player know which cards you purchased...  

Based on my experiences, I am unable to see where's the enjoyment.  Surely these players don't play with the Fremen!  An utterly pointless game, yet I was wiling to give this one another try, as so many folks here appear to enjoy this game (for the life of me, I can't understand why).

Merits a 1, but the game oozes with theme, so it earns a 2 (most pointless 4 hour non-game I have ever seen).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A true classic in every sense of the word.

The design is dynamic, tense and filled with theme, to the point where every game seems so utterly different, but always memorable.  

It is a game where the players not only talk excitedly about the game mechanics, strategies and possibilities of play, but also about the events that happened, surprise upsets, victories and defeats.

It should be noted that the rules are not at all straight-forward, and choosing which rules to play with is a matter of experimenting.  

Also worth noting is that other players have posted lots of house rules that make a lot of sense : have a look at these and decide which to include.

The only negative point to this game is finding a copy : thanks to all those uploaders who spent so much time to allow the rest of us an opportunity to play this amazing game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is an excellent game because of the interaction and role playing.  It fits well with the theme, and is fairly attractive.  I own the French version, and even though my playgroup does not have french speakers, the game is quite playable.  In fact, I think it adds to the exotic/gothic feel of the game. 

Down side to the game, it is definitely not for everyone.  Some of my group are frankly put-off by any science fiction theme.  Also, the game is lengthy and promotes back-stabbing.  

The Descartes version included the Spice Trader, and Duel expansions.  I like the spice trader for starting the game with theme, but find the Duel to be a tedious addition.

RATING: 6
Based on one play: Game ended on second turn. Seemed to play pretty decently with 6 (which is more than most games can say). Some potential but lots of questionable stuff that would require more plays to get a solid opinion on.

What happened in our play: on turn 2 a Nexus came out. Guild & Emperor aligned. Atreides, Harkonnen, and Fremen aligned. Bene Gessirit held the balance of power, threw in with Atreides et al. A couple of big battles and the 4-way alliance won at the end of the 2nd turn (with 0 spice harvested by anyone).

- The Bene Gessirit player can "predict" which player will win on which turn. That's a bit of an ask for players with little/no experience in the game. "Do most games go 5 turns or 10 turns?"

- Blind bidding (except for Atreides) on the treachery cards feels like it might be a bit too much randomness.

- Feels like there needs to be something to just prevent everyone from forming a 6-way alliance on the first Nexus and calling it Game Over. If winning in an alliance is okay, then why not a 6-way alliance? Why prefer a 2-way over a 6-way if you're going to share the victory?

Still, I'd be curious to play a few more times and see how else the game can develop. My provisional rating could go up.

RATING: 7
Great theme that fits the actions very well - every faction plays very different (if very unbalanced), a lot of backstabbing and insane vicious battles, and a very harsh unforgiving environment.

The initial infatuation is definitely starting to wear off, but I can't help wanting to play it anyway.  The problems are that we have more than one run into a deadlock-type situation.  With no alliances and more than 3 players it's impossible to win (and even with 3, only if someone totally ignores your attempt at victory).  With alliances, again a stalemate was reached.  Holding out for more plays :)

Update: It is a very long game, with quite a few complex rules, so it requires a serious commitment before pulling out. Downgraded to 7.

RATING: 10
Now out of print, I played a friend's version of the came hand-made from someone in Ukraine. It was beautiful, though I can't compliment the original on that aspect. What I can say is for a game of area control, the amount of assymetry in the player powers was staggering. Yet not debilitating. In fact, the spread seemed almost necessary, as their interdependence made the world run - just like in the books. An excellent adherence to theme, storms and worms must be avoids, just as the Holtzmann Effect with shields and lasers must be! Play as the Bene Gesserit and win by being the power behind the throne, the Fremen by keeping Dune Arrakis, or the Emperor by spreading your might across the land. Truly a pinnacle of combining theme and mechanics.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Dune seems like it was pretty ahead of its time.  I especially love the little mini-economy in the game.  If you want to buy influence, pay the Emperor player; if you want to ship units, pay the Guild player.  The special powers all mesh nicely while fitting the theme incredibly well and varying pretty drastically.  The addition of negotiating, bribery, and functional alliances are of course always a plus for me.  The only unfortunate downfalls to the game are the length and vague rules (I had to abort my first game due to a misreading that locked the progress and broke the ending conditions).  Still, if you want a negotiating game with special powers, varied ending conditions for the players, and a conquest type feel - this old dinosaur is probably a decent bet.  It really doesn't feel as dated as it should.

Ideal Players: 6

Pro: Perfectly captures the feel of Frank Herbert's Dune. Each game tells an epic story. Deterministic combat with a blind-bidding system. Players each have different abilities and special victory conditions, leading to a dynamic game board and a different game every time.

Con: Requires 6 players for a good game. Expansions make the game too complex.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.8
I fell in love with the assymetric powers of the races, the evolving alliances and the general gameplay.

Apparently, the original rules were someplace between unstable and and unplayable but the adopted homerules with double spice flow make the game awesome.

Battle is not a simple dice roles plus modifiers.  Each faction has a option to play a weapon and a defense that may block a weapon.  In addition, the number of troops committed to a battle can be supported by Spice which can double their affect.  A leader is added and he adds to the faction's strength -- unless, he is a TRAITOR.

Truely a fun game.  A little long and really is best with 6 players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Areacontrol:bluffing:negotiations:alliances:traitors:auctions:variable player powers:

initial rating 1 play 6 players, don't expect any movement

[b]WOW[/b] Epic epic game and so far ahead of its time. This very unique game actually deserves it accolades. The game is bursting with THEME, maybe the most themactic game I have played, fantastic.

The game is EVIL, just like the movie/books, love it! One of the best multiplayer games I have played, it incorporates a lot of different elements and does it well. Tons of special powers, very cool combat system, alliances, negotiations, all very cool stuff. 

There are three big negatives, length of course, a game could play in 2 hours but could also run very very long, had this been a 3 hour game there sure would be more '10' ratings. Rules, I sure am glad we had an experienced player running our game, lots of rules and exceptions that will make picking the game up and playing hard after a layover. Randomness, the order cards come out (especially the worm and destruction of the wall) determine how the game will be shaped and there is no determining that. Balance is questionable also as two factions are constantly collecting spice (money) and always have money to outbid people for the cards, ship more men, etc. This is where alliances can hopefully come in depending on the worm of course.

Despite these negatives, which can be somewhat overcome with more play, this is a fantastic system that I look forward to playing again

RATING: 9
The spice must flow and sometimes blood. An oldie but a good game. The main win condition is to control a certain amount of important stronghold locations on arrakis. Doing so you're trying to avoid sand storms, collect spice, form alliances, and backstab friends and family.

For first time players its probably best to limit alliances to 2 people unless you want a really short game as inexperienced players will just want to gang up. The fremen can be difficult to play for new players as well.

Overall a fun game with treachery and backstabbing that can last anywhere from 2 hours to an entire day if you use more advanced rules or play the optional no alliances rule.

RATING: 7
2011-10-04 

Initial Rating: 9.5 (September 2011)

This is the game I first thought Cosmic Encounter (which disappointed me) should be. GREAT game. Surely with more plays this will crack my top ten of all-time favorite games :D.	

You want negotiation you’ve got it, you want betrayal, you have that too (you have also alliances, traitors, bluffing and much more). 

The gameplay is awesome (there are no dice). You need to play a different strategy according to your faction and you are so immersed in the game you don’t feel any downtime between turns. Very nice :D.

The game is a little complex (I like that) thus it can take a long time to explain the rules to new players (but is totally worth it).

The theme is incredible rich and accurate, fans of the Dune novel should play this right away (sadly, I have not read the book yet but all my fellow players have and they were ecstatic about that).

On the bad side I think the game needs 6 players to truly shine and Dune can take a long time, also the components can be hard to handle as they are very small.

Definitively my kind of game.

If you can get a copy of this DO IT! you won´t regret it.

Current Rating: 9.5

2015-09-15

Dune was in my Top Ten favorite games for years. I love the game and I will get a copy one way or another. It is a shame FFG couldn’t reprint it.  After I played Dune I read the novels and the game theme does justice to it.

Dune´s playtime can be very long or very short, if you play with less than 6 players the game can finish very quickly due to the alliances. Nowadays we always play that the alliance can be formed by two players only. If we allow 3 players alliances the game will finish very soon. 

As I´m currently doing my list of Top 100 games of all time, I have to downgrade Dune´s rating to denote my current feelings for the game. It is still a very good game, furthermore if you have into account the year it was published. I love it.

**NOSTALGIA RATING**

This...this is a classic!

I loved the first novel of the series (it's one of my favorite Sci-Fi books ever) and this game catches the theme so very well.

Duplicitous bastards conniving against you, brilliant play, alliances, battles...a classic.

Long, but worth it.

I sold my Avalon Hill version, bought the French (for the expansions) and am going to make a wood board for it in the future.

By today's standards, I'd clock it at about an 8...so not that much off actually, damn it was good back then (still is actually).

RATING: 8
Race to control Arakis through troops that are paid for based on spice haul and/or CHOAM charity - though beware the traitors in your faction. Follows the books fairly well, but what makes this game stand out is the battling mechanic: you hide how much you are wagering and which leader adds their power until it's time to show, and you can add one weapon and defence in a one-shot try to negate the other's power.

BIG PLUS: battle mechanic, long rules but simple to play, follows the book
BIG MINUS: really works best with more players

MEGA MINUS: this game is not in print any more… and it should be!!!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is one of the sacred cows of gaming, and I've seen it slaughtered quite a bit on BGG. Some of the innovations in the game haven't aged very well, and the rules are a bit of a mess with all their exceptions and ambiguities, so there's fair grounds for criticism. Nevertheless, it's a majestic fusion of mechanics and theme. Everything--even the clunky parts--seems to fit the Dune universe remarkably well. 

I know we're just starting to scratch the surface of what's possible in the metagame. Bribery, pseudo-alliances, threats, backstabbing...the game is fertile territory for all of it, and when you're fumbling through the first couple times it's hard to know the relative value of territories, information, and the use of each faction's special abilities. I can read Pug's excellent series of strategy guides and understand them much better now. The alliance wins do seem a bit deflating, but it's up to everyone else to band together and get in the way, something we didn't do (or even cajole each other to try). 

Flawed? Yes, definitely by today's standards. Ugly? The original game, sure--that's why I built my own. But I won't be able to get the game off my mind anytime soon, and I'm itching to play again, because I'm certain it'll get even better with repetition.

RATING: 10
Rare: a uniquely happy marriage of narrative and mechanics. I can't imagine what it would be like to play not knowing the Dune universe. Overly complex. Dozens of plays in and we still get the rules wrong. Six players all but mandatory. But the design keeps giving (and giving in, to story). Here imbalance is virtue. Six factions that resist and enable each other in delightful ways. The Bene Gesserit may never win, but never-winning has never been so fun. Defeat here has the glow of defeat in narrative: somehow it's more interesting, more vital. Games need drama. The story of numbers adds up to nothing. Full disclosure: I hated math until I discovered it had imaginary numbers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This game is incredible. It fully captures the harsh feel of Arrakis. Battles are bloody (annihilation of both sides is not uncommon), assassination of your characters is unavoidable, and no city is truly safe. We find the game has a strong theme of biding your time then pouncing with full force, but while some races start with a stronger map presence than others, all will get their chance at glory!

The game shows off some fantastic theme from the Dune universe (if a bit of 70s camp/kitsch), and staying true to the story every faction plays very differently and with real character. Its much better to play with 6 players to really get the full flavour and inter-relationship between the very diverse factions.

This is a real hard core war and politics game: if you take it seriously a game should take at least 5 hours. Not for the faint of heart. Because of this, it can be a little AP prone, but the experience is worth it!

If I were to change anything about the game, it would be to make the victory conditions a little less focussed around control of cities, and I would like to see the alliance rules a bit clearer.

RATING: 9
First game of Dune! I was done for as Harkonnen by turn two--over-aggressive play in the beginning seems like a good thing with these guys, but it needs to be balanced with smart use of spice. Oh, and don't leave Carthag vulnerable to counterattacks (idiot).

Honestly, despite the fact that I spent much of the game licking my wounds, I can't stop thinking about how I would've played differently given more experience (hide out in the Polar Sink for while, just biding my time...then BOOM, take advantage of Carthag's extra movement and wipe those bastard Fremen off the map!...maybe). 

This really seems like a game to grow into.  And now that I've played I don't find the rules overhead nearly as intimidating.  I'm actually looking forward to teaching this one and getting everyone into it.  Oh, and I call Guild next time.

RATING: 7
Thematically it is very strong and I see why some people like this game, but I couldn't enjoy it. Everything revolves around quick combats which can be devastating even if you win. Every round people blindly bid for cards - very random. Also, I don't like the movement constrictions and the lack of resource grabbing options (some factions constantly get free money but spice itself only appears into one place per round).
The idea of a circular board around which a storm moves is quite neat though and the combat mechanics are okay. I'd still rather play Dominant Species or Game of Thrones if I'm in the mood for a long heavy area movement game.

RATING: 4
There are interesting ideas and mechanics. But in over all, I don't like much the game. I played with the spice harvest expansion.

The spice harvest is a pretty cool mechanic, but I would have prefered using it during each of the game's turn, not as a game setup minigame.

Unless you play emperor or guild, you are always short in money because spice cost so much to acquire that it actually does not worth it to try capture spice. So I would have loved to use the spice havest rules each turn so that you could have more diplomatic influence during the game on other players and so that there could be a minimum income of spice that enters the game.

Also the map is so big for the limited movements you are allowed to do in a turn. So maybe with a smaller map and more mobility, it would have been more interesting.

I would have loved to have the feeling of "push your luck". Do you risk collecting spice, do you stay to collect more or should you wait for a better opportunity. You don't get that feeling when playing dune

So there is place for improvement. I am curious to see the new FFG version.

RATING: 9
This game is pretty brilliant, actually. The rules seem a little convoluted at first, but they actually only take up half a dozen pages, and after the first few turns play flows quite smoothly. The strategy required is pretty deep, but not brain-busting. What's most impressive, though, is how the Magic the Gathering/Cosmic Encounter-style rules exceptions mirror the traits of actual races and characters in the book, while at the same time keeping the game remarkably well-balanced. By the end of my first play-through, I was frankly in awe of the designer for having pulled this off. My only complaint is that the alliances mechanic makes the game a little too prone to sudden reversals when you're playing with fewer than five participants.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.8
This game is genius and some of the most saturated gaming enjoyment to be had. The overall game is pretty simple in the rules, but shockingly intricate and flexible in strategy and possibilities. No two games are going to feel the same, despite the same set-up. (contrast this with a game like Axis&Allies, where the set-up is always the same and the game typically starts and unfolds the same way, dictated really by how the dice of the battles unfold). Dune isn't going to feel different because lack of dice, but more so based on player choices and interactions. 
The game flys by. You don't feel the downtime, even when playing with the Duel expansion (where the game will stop as two players duel each other). 
Rich in strategy, deception, intrigue and player interaction. The game is extremely fluid; the balance of the game can dramatically shift from turn to turn and even within the turn, so you have to stay on your toes, constantly reassessing possible alliances, bribes, challenges and priorities. 
The duel expansion was great fun. It added a pretty tense and suspensful element to the game that even the invested spectators feel. You have to choose which side you think will win; if you chose correctly, you win some much needed spice. If you loose, you loose the leader you sent to support the combantant. 
The game and some of the cards offer some very open and creative ways of engaging the game; Kanly (for challenging duels). Declare a specific action and a player, if it happens, you duel.
"If the Guild ship a troop to the Funeral Flats, we duel". 
The truth serum card is also fabulous; you force an opponent to answer a Yes/No question. "Are you going to attach the Fremen this turn" 
Its the little elements like this, plus the unique faction advantages, the low reliance on randomness for battle, and the amount of player interaction that affrds this game to be one of the best. 

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Seems to be quite as good as people here on the Geek make it out to be. For a game from the dark seventies, it's simply amazing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[b]First impression of the game?[/b]
Fun game. It's cool to play an old classic and experience some of the game mechanics that are still influencing games today.

[b]What are the highlights?[/b]
There are a lot of nifty little ditties in this game. The traitor element can be devastating and significantly affects decision-making. The rotating storm adds uncertainty and a need for caution. The alliances add a great deal of variability.

[b]What are the drawbacks?[/b]
The nature of the mechanics creates some ambiguities which requires a lot of rules lookup. Some of the faction powers seem unbalanced but I could be convinced otherwise with more plays. Both of our initial plays (11/14/09) ended with a Harkonnen/Empire alliance victory.

[b]Who would enjoy this game?[/b]
Obviously people who love the Dune universe of Frank Herbert's books are going to eat this up. However, I don't believe knowledge of the books or movies is a prerequisite. 

[b]Who would [i]not[/i] enjoy this game?[/b]
This is definitely a gamers' game. Game length (2 - 4 hour) and potential rules-lawyering prevents this from being a good family game.

[b]Would you play this game again?[/b]
Yes, I could definitely see playing this game once or twice a year. It is a neat little game with a lot of thematic elements that I like in games like Fury of Dracula with combat and negotiation components of old-style Ameritrash games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Just a note, I knew I was going to love the game just by reading the rules.

I punched out my new but not so new copy of Dune and have never looked back. The game has everything I expect from a game.

Variable Player Powers - The 6 different factions make for a game where each player has a unique set of abilities to draw from within the game. This also fits perfectly within the theme of the novel. 

Treacherous Backstabbing Harkonnens 
Subtle but Brutal Bene Gesserit
Native Stillsuit-Wearing Fremen
Guild and their Shipping Monopoly
Prescient Atreides
Mighty Emperor

Negotiation, Extortion, Bribing - The game cries out for alliances. The player who can wheel and deal the best will see themselves on the profiting end of things.

Diceless Combat - The battle system simply works. There's a great deal of thought that can be put into every battle. Are you going to dial up your troops? Are you going to risk a strong leader? What are the odds of your opponent having this defense/offense? 

Mindgames - In Dune, you play against your enemies as much as the game itself. There's lots of room to bluff and coerce other players into doing your bidding.

RATING: 7
4 / 5 - Great.

This is a mad game. I'm not aware of any other title that screws without your head quite so much in terms of feeding drips of information to individual players and making everyone excruciatingly paranoid. Every decision you make is a horrible combination of mechanical strategy (no dice, remember) and hidden information: the tension is palpable. Indeed if anything the game is almost too tense. The legendary manner in which the game connects to the source material hardly needs further discussion and is well deserved. However the near-requirement for 5-6 players for a satisfying session is a shame.

RATING: 10
Full Game Review available in game forum.

When you have exactly 6 players and enough time to finish playing, this is simply the best game I've ever played. The pace is slow, the decisions hard, and your gaming group will need to come up with some measures for dealing with these elements to suit you.

That being said, the Storm mechanic is one of the best ever created, the theme is great, and the asymmetry is extremely well done (unless you have less than 6 players, then the game becomes terrible). A lot of newer games are using modified versions of the combat system, which is a good thing.

Extremely frustrating until you know what you are doing and how everything works, but once you get it the payoff is big.

RATING: 8
It would be easy to point out Dunes so called flaws compared to modern designer board games if you wanted to approach it like that. Dune is not pretty, it's unforgiving, rules can be a handful, playing time is unpredictable and so on. But if you are able to look past that you'll find a game that offers something its modern counterparts do not. The variable player powers in Dune are variable indeed like in nowhere else and powerful with a punch, yet suprisingly balanced. Negotiations and alliances rule over combat, which itself is agonizing fun. And the setting of course is lovely and perfectly executed. The spice must flow.

RATING: 8.5
First play confirms, this game lives up to its reputation. In our four player game, it was an absolute smack-down knife fight to the end, climaxing in round six between myself as Fremen and another player as Atreides. Vicious combat, complex metagame (auctions, bribery, alliances, backstabs) quirky special powers that remind me of Cosmic Encounter (gee, wonder why?) Hesitant to rate it higher than this for now, but maybe future plays will allow me to acknowledge its superiority.

------------

Second visit to the desert planet was absolutely brutal - ended up winning with a three-way alliance. It's an interesting game, but I feel like things don't really get moving until the first worm appearance and the nexus it causes. In this case, it was two hours into game play. There doesn't seem to be a good way to fix this - if you intentionally dealt a Shai Hulud card every four or five cards, than players would plan accordingly and some of the dramatic tension of one showing up would be lost.

Also during this play we played with a few rules wrong, which completely and totally changed the game - we got Bene Gesserit unit placement wrong, and space landing wrong. Next time should be an even better game.

RATING: 7.75
Works best with full complement of players, however the game can be anti-climatic with a premature allied victory.  Conversely, the game can drag on in a protracted stalemate with paritied alliances.  The game is showing its age, though, as the components are underwhelming.  The map is way too small for a 6 player game; the tokens are difficult to handle and place inside the tiny territories.  The perforated tiny cards are a nightmare to shuffle and get damaged easily.  Consequently, this game would benefit greatly from an update/reprint.

RATING: 6
My relation to dune is ... complicated. I want to like, because it has some interesting mechanics and my friends love this game.
Yet I it still did not click with me. Why? Hard to say. I like the game offers you a foundation for diplomacy, treaties and betrayal, but I do not like that you have to wait for an event to build or break treaties.
Having to sacrifice troops for fighting power leads to tough choices, but I feel they are underpowerd compared to the leaders. Also I hate the fact they are only worth half a power point. Would it be so complicated to make whole numbers?
The game basicaly eliminates chance, which is good and bad. Because you have to pay attention all the time! I want to play to have fun, but the level of commitment Dune requires feels tiring, leading to missing or forgetting important information, leading to defeat and being yelled at be your treaty partner.

RATING: 5
The most striking aspect of the board game is the theme recreation, cleverly designed to reflect the Dune universe. Fans will appreciate the many details included there; the player abilities, the map, the turn process, evertyhing makes sense. At the end of the day, it's an area control game with abstract victory conditions, but during the gameplay you will feel you are representing your family, etc.

Another great feature of Dune is the inequality of the races. It's not that they have different stats or posess some loose exclusive skill. They are REALLY different. And their differences are VERY powerul and have a HUGE impact in the game. This has been said about many games, but in Dune it's more true than in any other: the game experience and strategies change completely depending on who you play with.

There are some aspects where the game shows its age, though, like the player elimination. It's a feature present in very few of the recent games, and certainly in no "hours-long" game. It is indeed a problem, because in most instances, players need to be eliminated in order for someone to win. And being eliminated when there's still more than one hour to go is not nice.

I have not played enough to have a strong opinion about this game and my game experience was "contaminated" by the inclusion of its 2 expansions. It's not a game up my alley, but it has enough attractiveness to be played once per year.

PS: I owned a copy of Rex: Final Days of an Empire, but I immediately sold it. The theme is so rich that it doesn't make sense to play it outside the Dune universe.

First impression [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/3863204#item3863204]here[/url].

RATING: 7
Preliminary rating after 2 plays (5p), French edition. 

A game that meets all my expectations, that's for sure. It mechanically feels A LOT like Cosmic Encounter, the atmosphere is very different though.
I won't deny that we may have missed or mixed up rules to some extent, but the game seems to come with a truely fundamental defect resulting from the alliance victory option that is offering no incentives for expelling any player from an alliance or for a strong fraction to ally with a weaker one. House rule essentially required, otherwise simply broken. Could be an 8 or 9 if the issue was fixed.

House rule idea: A maximum of 2 fractions may constitute an alliance.

RATING: 6
Thematically superb. But ... there aren't that many decisions to make in the game; its not continually engaging. You do the best you can with your power. The strength of these powers (and the ability to shoot for victory) waxes and wanes, and therefore for reasonable periods when you don't have a shot at it, there's not much of interest to do. However, the quality and importance of the decisions you make in Dune are crucial, as opposed to many multi-player games where there numerous engaging decisions to make through the game, but few in themselves are crucial. The big attraction is the alliance capability with its scope for diplomacy and it's prerequisite setup to bring something to the negotiation table. I'm happy enough to play if others want to explore, as long as everyone's committed to keeping the game moving.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
If I had to sum this game up quickly, I suppose the best comment would be "Fun, but frustrating."  Dune introduced (well, introduced them to me, at least) a number of interesting ideas and mechanics that I've seen appear in later games.  It also does an interesting job of capturing the flavor of the different factions that feature in the books.  There's also good strategy and tactics to satisfy the serious gamer.

If the game "falls down," it's due to the interaction of different victory conditions and factions.  It's amazingly annoying (to many, anyway) to win the game as the Harkonnens on turn 4, only to find out that the Bene Gesserit had predicted that win and therefore win instead of you.  For some in our group, these types of interactions kill the joy in the game and leave it on the shelf (far too frequently, if you ask me).

But I like it and it usually gets a few votes when it's time to pick a game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The amazingly beautiful fan redesign by Ilya.

Clanky, complicated, unbalanced, ambiguous (with all the different variant rules out there), hard to learn and get into (your first play is likely going to be frustrating), can end on turn 1 or drag to turn 15, erratic and volatile (one round of bidding can change the power dynamics), unforgiving and the special powers of the factions really narrows down the paths to victory to each faction.

BUT oh so epic, and a terrific implementation of the theme. With the beautiful fan-made version, this game is really an intense experience that makes or breaks your day.

Finalmente ho giocato a Dune, la leggenda, il mito senza tempo, il Kwisatz Haderach dei giochi da tavolo, e ne sono rimasto soddisfatto.
Innanzitutto è talmente coinvolgente e calato nell'ambientazione che sembra quasi un gioco di ruolo (ah, l'Atomique de Famille!). Le regole sono cucite addosso alle sei razze, e le abilità assegnate sono così pesanti nell'economia della partita che c'è una strategia diversa per ogni gruppo scelto. 
Inoltre, ho giocato a pochi giochi con così tanta interazione tra i giocatori. Il sistema di gioco è al servizio dell'ambientazione: i traditori, le minacce e i duelli rendono bene l'atmosfera da "fantascienza feudale" che pervade il libro (e il film), la tempesta di Coriolis perenne e le insidie del deserto ben rappresentano Arrakis.
Le battaglie - senza dado, un caso più unico che raro - sono originalissime.
Infine, le molteplici condizioni di vittoria, che cambiano a seconda della razza e dell'alleanza, mettono ancora più pepe a un gioco che di per se' è già molto speziato.
Per ultimo, i (pochi) difetti:
- Dura tantissimo: suppongo che un tavolo di giocatori esperti faccia lievitare ulteriormente i tempi.
- Regole difficili: il primo impatto col gioco è devastante. A mio parere le regole sentono un po' i morsi del tempo.
- Componenti non all'altezza: dagli anni '80 a oggi la grafica nei boardgame ha fatto passi da gigante. La rotella per i combattimenti è "gratuita" :)
- Bilanciamento delle razze: a occhio sembra che qualcuno sia più svantaggiato degli altri.
