RATING: 8.25
Fun Factor: 10
Components: 7 (Components are what you would expect from a game released in 1979.)
Theme: 10 (If you are familiar with the books, then you know that this game is absolutely married to the theme.)
Mechanics: 6 (The rules are a total bust- very difficult to decipher and high mental cost of entry)
Theme + Mechanics: 9
Replay Value: 10
Scaling/Players: 5 (Poor. Really no good with less than four players.)
Subjective +0.25 (This game is possibly the most elegant multiplayer themed wargame ever designed. The amount of player interaction in this game is nothing short of amazing, very few games approach it with the "nexus" mechanic and treachery bidding, along with special powers.)  Edit: subjective rating reduced 0.25 because of problems of length and metagame tendencies.

Edit (1-1-2013): This game suffers some issues with the metagame.  When playing with money factions i.e. Emperor and Guild, if all the other players are not present the game suffers certain weaknesses.

Another nearly perfect game.  The theme of the game is so well supported by the mechanics that you will become fully immersed in the game...  Excellent with a full compliment of players, nearly useless when you have less than 4 players.  Not too short, not too long, this game is really deserving of a full review on my part.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.7
Dune is not just a game, it is a life event.  If you can get six people together for an indeterminate amount of time this can be one of the greatest uses of time ever devised.  

I may be partial because I have read and loved the books multiple times throughout my life, but somehow they captured the personality of the various factions in the mechanic of the game itself.  Harkonnen is vile and treacherous.  The Emperor is rich yet flawed, his power slowly fading throughout the game.  The Atreides are prescient, all-knowing and intimidating foes.  The Spacing Guild is controlling movement throughout the board and profiting off the conflict of others.  The Fremen are wandering native across the board, using the storm and worm to their advantage.  And all along, the Bene Gesserit are manipulating everything behind the scenes.  

The more the players know the game, the more the game melts away and you are in the world, an agent of Arrakis with your own motives and glory.  Trust your ally, but only so far.  Maneuver, entice your enemy to overextend, throw the battle to win the war.  Rarely has inspiration and creativity been given such free reign in a board game as here.

And when the game is over, blink and look around and return slowly to your normal life.  But not really.

RATING: 9
Excellent game designed by EON; the people that designed Cosmic Encounter.  They altered the design and included some of the flavor of Dune. The game is almost a serious version of CE. Very deep strategies. The game, along with the 2 expansions is still produced by Eurogames. It is the best &#039;wargame&#039; around. The variety and depth are unmatched; there is combat, diplomacy, auctions, area control and variable victory conditions.  The systems all work well together.  It is a great game. Only drawback is that it requires 5 or 6 players for the best games and that increases the playing time significantly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
The theme of this game is really impressive. The design of the print and play version I had by Ilya Baranovsky is a sight to behold. The mechanics are so much smoother than it's child, Rex that it's not even funny. This game suffers a little bit from it's age. Lots of the battles wind up being harsher than they need to be thanks to betrayals. The economics are so tight that if you aren't a faction that gets money through the actions of the other factions, you can easily land yourself in an untenable situation. Battles are very costly, but the game gives you very little to do other than battle.

This game is so much player negotiation that despite it being a really cool game, it was completely incompatible with my group.

:star: Art and components
:nostar: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:nostar: Good with different player counts
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:nostar::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Well, one of the best games ever made, this game is a truly gem in my collection. Unlike much of other strategy games this one is normally done in a few hours (means: between 2 and 3). In my group it is normally played as a warm-up before we start on heavier stuff. I will just describe two fascinating features of the game, which are different compared to other games. First, all players play races with race-specific different strongs and weaknesses, means that every race is uniquely played (its even not like a credit point system, like race 1 is good in A and B but poor in C and race 2 is poor in A but good in B and C ... they are COMPLETELY different. The second feature is the quick altering of the game, meaning that in minutes the game can topple and a player, who was minutes before nearly out of play, could challenge to win the game.

RATING: 3.5
The game captures the feel of the novel better than any other alternate medium (movies, etc.).  At least it felt that way for a while.

This game is really not that good in my opinion.  The winning conditions are ludicrous and non-thematic.  The game is so fragile that if a players are merely playing to win, rather than role-play Dune, there's little reason for the game to last past the first Shai-hulud.

It's fairly easy for all the players that control strongholds to ally together on the first Shai-hulud and end the game as equal winners.

If you want to implement personal agendas like, "I want a solo win," or "I will only ally with the Fremen," then things can get interesting but the mechanisms aren't implemented make such actions conducive to winning.  

You can implement house rules, I suppose, to fix the game, otherwise it's as I said, more of a role playing experience than a game.

RATING: 9
The rules set bounds for an incredible narrative that the players exist freely in.  Players are not playing against the game, but against each other's personalities augmented by their faction characteristics.  There is no other game which exhibits such freedom without becoming dominated by a strict rules set. True, the rules are a bit of a maze to navigate as each faction has many rules exceptions, but as the game progresses, these exceptions become obvious and expected.  This is the one of my favorite games that I have played this year and may possible become my favorite game of all time.  The theme, mechanics, and rules mesh together to make a truly spectacular game!  It is a must buy for anyone who has read the books, loves diplomatic games, and wants to make plans within plans within plans.

Currently making a homemade copy... tons of work and not cheap!

RATING: 10
My favourite game.  I have a spare copy for when the first set wears out.  There are problems with the rules, and I don't like the expansions much, but the base game perfectly meshes playing the other players with playing a system.  This is not a game with a perfect plan, it is a game where plans reflect plans that reflect plans.  This is a wargame and is too complex to be an acceptable family strategy game.  At its best with 5 or 6 experienced players and a few house rules to ensure it plays in 2-4 hours.  It brilliantly solves the multi-player leader hitting problem by allowing alliances to win, and controlling when they can form and break.  It is asymmetric but balanced.  Undimmed by the Eurogame invasion, the game is actually fairly simple rules-wise and offers an unparalleled gaming experience.

RATING: 10
Dune has to be the only book whose finest adaptation is a board game. Like the book it's based on, Dune is filled with hidden motives, shifting alliances, and unforeseen outcomes. As an interpretation of Frank Herbert's original novel, it is impeccable. As a board game experience, it is filled with opportunities for creative play and secret plans. Mechanically it plays a little like a heavily-themed "advanced" Cosmic Encounter, but in practice it feels very different. It's a six-player experience that is unmatched, and it's a great showcase of how well board games can embody the themes of famous novels.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The 'million ways to play Dune' stemming from this being a relic of '70s game design and antiquated rules and rules-writing is a turn-off. Rex is more streamlined and clearer in every aspect, and even if it's off the mark in some places, at least it sticks to one principle.

But the natural evocation of the theme here just works.

Biggest difference is in movement and shipping, and I think this is more a case of preferring whichever system you learned first (Dune or Rex).

[u]Key Differences[/u]
 - More faction powers in Dune. Unfortunately, these are on the fiddly/powerful side: Atreides' Kwisatz Haderach, Harkonnen's Leader Capture, Guild's Maneuver Anytime. I favor keeping the additional Fremen powers. b{Advantage: Rex}b
 - Karama is too many abilities crammed onto a card. Better to have it streamlined into a Cosmic Zap or similarly pithy card (it's a Cosmic Zap with exceptions + faction-specific abilities + 2 generic abilities), which Rex did. b{Advantage: Rex}b
 - Maneuver/Shipping differences: Rex is more dynamic, while Dune = very restricted by whichever strongholds aren't still occupied, and the importance of movement discourages combat and losing the tokens that took a while to develop. b{Advantage: Rex}b
 - I welcome any structure on diplomacy/bribery besides free-for-all. The additional structure actually encourages people to make deals (instead of just relying on a person's word). OTOH, easily ported to Rex. r{Advantage: Dune}r
 - While leader-revivals in Dune make combat even scarier, I prefer leader death to mean something. r{Advantage: Dune}r
 - Winner takes all the spice from killed leaders in Dune while killing leaders gains influence in Rex. I favor the latter since the former results in more separation between winners and losers and makes combat scarier. b{Advantage: Rex}b
 - Increased revival rate in Rex makes combat less scary but also nerfs the Fremen. A wash since it's also needed to balance out Double Spice Blows without Advanced Combat, and get more combat into an 8-round game.
 - Treachery Deck in Dune encourages creativity with Truthtrance and combat with Worthless, while Strategy Deck in Rex provides flexibility with each card having 2 uses. r{Advantage: Dune}r

Overall, combat is less scary in Rex, which is welcome, because blind-bidding combat is already as scary as it can get. I'm concerned that combat in Dune is scary to the point of turtling.

4+4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75
Avalon Hill did a wonderful job adapting the supremely complex world that Herbert conceived into a beautiful, playable game complete with spice and worms. If you loved the book or even just learned of Dune when Sting popularized the movie, you'll still enjoy this compelling game. It's best when played with four or more opponents, but be prepared for a lengthy endeavor; Dune takes many, many hours to complete. Some of that is due to the complexity of the rules, but our lengthiest games were always those with the most experienced gamers. If you have the time and enjoy fantasy war gaming set in space (i.e. Cosmic Encounter or Twilight Imperium) then I'd highly recommend Dune. For the casual gamer with little familiarity with Herbert's classic novel or with a low tolerability for complex, lengthy games this wouldn't be the best place to start.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Wow. What a game! Made in 1979, Dune has alot of mechanics and ideas that are way ahead of its time. Player abilities that interacts creatively, negotiation, an auction for special cards, combat that involves secret assigning of troops, leaders and special cards. Amazing.

The object of the game betrays AH's wargaming roots: capture 3 of 5 strongholds. But the turn order is fairly brisk and streamlines (players can only place units in 1 location and only move 1 set of troops). This makes decisions agonising and tense. Battles are also tense affairs and one must be careful as to when and whom to attack. The negotiation and alliance aspect has touches of Diplomacy and Cosmic Encounter.

The ruleset and variety of card and player effects leaves room for debate and our group saw some vaguely nasty disagreements at times.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]Best 6 Player Game Award[/b]

Fantastic game. I've played the reimagination, Rex, more than I've played this, but I feel that the games are similar enough that I have a good idea of how the system works. 

As for Rex VS Dune, my impression so far is that it feels like the same game but it's not, and that Dune simply has more stuff -- more powers, more cards, more strategies. Which appeals to my gaming tastes, so I'm rating this higher, though Rex seems to be successful in its goal of streamlining the components and rules -- especially the rules, which are all over the place with Dune (seems like no group plays the same way!)

First impression preferences:

*I like the "more stuff" factor in Dune.

*I like the shipping rules in Rex and the super-mobility. However, I also like the increased emphasis on movement in Dune. So I appreciate them differently.

*Worthless cards are pretty damn fun for Atreides and Bene Gesserit, annoyance for everyone else. Karama is cool.

*I think I like money gains for anyone who kills a leader in Rex, not just the winner. Dune battle losses are already harsher than Rex in multiple ways -- so it seems unnecessary to me to punish a losing player even further by letting the winner collect spice from his OWN leader!

*Rex is prettier and has some extra components that help and stand out more (the Sol fleet is much easier to spot than Storm, Bene/Xxxcha get tokens for their predictions, etc) and the map is easier for me to read; but there are some clean looking Dune redesigns out there

(5 Design + 5 Enjoyment)

RATING: 10
Has so many fantastic factors that all fit together in way that is just plain rip-roarin, memory-makin fun. To name just a few of the awesome moving parts:

blind bidding for treachery cards - some are completely useless, but your opponents dont know if you got a "trip to Gamont" crap card or if you've received a useful poison weapon or lasegun. The Harkonen player excells here, as he has cornered the market on Treachery. But, all payment for these cards goes straight to the Emperor player. 

The best variable player powers you will ever see in any game - like the clairvoyant Atreides being able to see part of your battle plan, or the Bene Gesseret and her "voice" that makes you do things against your will; or the Fremen's uncanny knowledge of the storms and Worm.

Speaking of Worms - they pop up when you don't want them to, and can devour entire legions of your troops, unless of course, you are the Fremen character and can ride them to any sand space on the board...

There are both troop movements on the board, and troop shipment onto the planet from off-world. The latter is costly, and paid to the Spacing Guild player. The Emporer player must at start ship all of his troops from off-world, but he has so much money he can afford it. 

Turn order is cyclical, but the starting player is determined by a storm that forever circles around the border of the board, destroying the best laid plans as it rages from one sector to the next.

Area control is important, as one must claim strongholds to win - not an easy task.

And lets not forget all the glorious battles - so much edge of your seat fun here, and with no dice rolling at all - just an elegant, easy to learn system involving choosing your leader, sacrificing as many troops as you can afford to, and picking attacks and defenses. So, so good. 

Spice is the currency of the game, and most players will have to fight for it. 

Honestly, once the players all understand their special powers and the mechanics of the game, it plays out like a symphony. Only drawback is that it must be played with at least 4 - the more the better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
My six rating is an uneasy compromise between my great admiration for the design and my disinclination to actually play it. Dune is variable player powers done right, alliances done right: every character feels very different, and powers are so complementary that different alliances are valuable at different times. Everything ties together very neatly.

The key is that you need the right group -- people have to play to win and they have to be happy to break alliances to do so. Helping your current allies can be dangerous because they may not be allies for long. You can't trust anyone.

You can see the way the game is *supposed* to work: it's possible for a victory on any turn, so players should be on edge the whole time, working to ensure that they're part of the alliance that takes it (or the one that snatches sole control). You just need a bunch of scheming, backstabbing little Machiavellis. 

When it doesn't work, it's a tedious, 4+ hour back-and-forth. I'm not sure I want to take that risk.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
I LOVE this game.  The only down side is it can have balance issues depending upon how many are playing and which factions they are playing.  A great political intrigue/light war game that has the best combat mechanics I have ever seen in a game.  It also faithfully adheres to the books and even cleverly incorporates advanced rules that fit the houses perfectly.  It amazes me they were able to put all this together in such a pleasing and well-balanced package.  Although this game can be a little pricey and hard to find, it is worth it.  If you have read the books and this game looks at all interesting to you, then you MUST play it.  The only catch is you really need at least 4 players for this game to shine.  It's best with all 6 though.

RATING: 7
I am shocked at how well-loved this game is...I owned it along with basically every other AH box release in the late 70s/early 80s, and played it some.  The game is fun, with lots going on, and the flavor is definitely there.  But I remember the victory conditions slanting so that you pretty much had victory determined by the alliances that got made, with one big battle in the Imperial Basin or with Family Atomics and a storm clinching the 3rd stronghold for the winners.

Very true to the source, but never really grabbed me as a game.

UPDATE:  OK, played this game for the first time in 15+ years the other day.  It was a lot of fun.  I think we used to play with 4/6 (including the Shield Wall) needed for the win and no increase for alliances.  The group I played with used what I believe is a pretty common optional rule where you need 3/5 as a solo winner, 4/5 as two-player alliance, and 5 for any larger group.

I still think the combat tends to be a bit punitive for larger stacks, but I was quite impressed.

RATING: 8.5
This is a really impressive design that, despite being 40 years old, has not aged a day.  This shouldn't be surprising considering it comes from the design team of Cosmic Encounter.  The integration of theme and mechanics is top shelf, especially if you're familiar with the source material.

Still, this is a difficult game for me to rate because I've only played it once and it's definitely one that would blossom with experience.  The rules themselves are quite simple, so learning to play the game is easy; the nuanced and substantial differences between factions, however, make learning to play the game WELL is a challenge.  Once I get a better handle of it, and play some sessions with people who are all experienced, I can see this one becoming a top 10 game for me.

RATING: 6

It was a lost battle for the alliance with two, since the other one had a much bigger army (since, with one more player, they could afford leave little behind). The game was over after this - the alliance of two won.

Though the end was great (because of the major swing of sure-thing battle), overall I wasn't impressed at all. The possibility of an alliance that can lead everyone on it to victory is a bad thing.

After this I learned that there are some variants that make the alliance thing do not happen. But I have yet to play like this (since I don't own the game), so I will keep the average rating.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
It pains me to rate this so low, as I usually love this type of game, and when I researched this, I thought it sounded like an older version of [gameid=6472].  I was wrong.  It's an incredible mess.  Dune does theme well, so well that I would say it's the best conversion of ANY theme to a boardgame, whether book, movie, or imaginary universe.  However, it gets more things wrong than it does right.  Components are ugly, units are thrown away frivolously and yet absurdly limited, and the treachery cards are both crucially important and too random.  DON'T try the advanced rules until you are familiar with the basic rules, or at least have a player in your group who is well-versed in the intricacies of the basic rules.

There's an absolutely critical failure of Dune, though, which is bad enough to get its own paragraph.  The alliance system is a [u]horrendously[/u] misimplemented mess.  As written, it's a fun-spoiler of magnificent proportions.  There's no reason not to gang up with the two or three or even four other strongest players on the board when the first worm comes up.  Then, it is child's play to secure the three strongholds needed for victory between several players.  What's the point of playing if four of the six players are able to claim victory together?  To make it worse, a single-player victory is nigh impossible if players are keeping their eyes open.  If nobody forms alliances, the game devolves into pile on the leader.  You can house-rule the alliance system down into merely unfun territory. but it smacks of underdeveloped, overly group-dependent laziness.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've only gotten to play this legendary game a couple of times. I have to say, I came away with some mixed emotions. Most of the game is surprisingly simplistic, looking at the gameboard and reading about it here on the geek I expected it to be a lot harder to learn than it actually was. (That's a good thing!)

I love the asymmetric sides and the way that all of the different factions mess with the board, each in their own unique way. This design does the best job of capturing a theme that I've ever seen. For instance, check out how the Bene Gesserit faction works (summary from wikipedia): [b]The Bene Gesserit player may "coexist" with other players' units without causing a confrontation, and may command other players to use or not to use certain cards during combat (representing their use of "the Voice"). At the beginning of the game the Bene Gesserit player secretly records the name of another player and the turn at which they think that other player will win the game. If the Bene Gesserit correctly guesses who will win and when, they win the game instead.[/b]

Is that the most thematic special ability ever? Yes, yes it is.

As for what holds this back from a truly stellar rating, the rules assume a certain amount of self-policing by the player's to protect the experience. Modern gamers have unfortunately been somewhat trained to look for and exploit loopholes, which can make the game break down a bit. The rules allow alliances to share both powers and a win, so technically a player can join an alliance just before the game ends and provide a victory point to someone else who has a big lead get a share of victory they clearly didn't deserve. Worse, they can form one at the very beginning of the game and never even consider betraying the alliance because there is little reason to do so. This can be a problem because some of the faction special abilities can be pretty game-breaking when combined with the special abilities of another allied faction, which in turn can lead to a very lopsided game from the outset, particularly if players align quickly and the rest of the table doesn't react to it.

Another thing that can sink the game before it starts is that it relies rather heavily on players leveraging turn order to force later acting players to absorb the resource and action cost of preventing game end. While strictly speaking this isn't quite the same as the "everybody loses" mechanic found in a lot of competitive co-op games, it functionally can end up that way if a player being leveraged decides to just not participate, as a counter-threat. That may just be a natural part of negotiation games n general, I'm not sure...but I do know that if the players are overly sensitive to economic bullying or quick to sabotage the game, it falls apart very quickly.

At any rate, the game requires selfish play and everyone at the table to be attempting to hold each other back at all costs, or it will end before it even starts. Dune is one of those weird games that almost guarantees a terrible experience on your first couple of plays, but if you stick with it and can find a group that is psychologically wired to do what the game asks them to do, then it is easily one of the most enjoyable and thematic licensed games out there.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
The Dune world comes to life with all its attendant tension and paranoia.  Thematic immersion is the game's strong suit.  Political alliances and treachery create a thick top layer of tension and a haze of uncertainty veils plans within plans.  Asymmetric wealth, mobility, troops, etc, to start, but can be offset by alliances, weapons, defences, weather, spice blows, etc. The game is as good as remembered from years ago, but only with the full complement of six players.  Games have an epic feel and narrative.  Excellent experience.  Unfortunately, uncertain length of the game (it varies from 1-6 hours) and needing six players makes it only an occasional experience.

The game experience lived up to memories of games played long ago, but the original components did not.  The Treachery cards in the Avalon Hill versions lack any verbiage and require the use of a reference sheet.  Found that annoying enough to make replacement cards using Ilya's files.  Again using Ilya's files, the map and faction shields were soon replaced as well.  Only the battle wheels and leader discs are from the original game.

RATING: 9
Rating based on one play at the Gathering of Strangers in SLC.  Appears to be a multi-player wargame that has some of the issues of that genre; kingmaking, the potential for unbalanced alliances.  Has great Dune flavor, with each of the houses having highly thematic, interesting abilities.  The game ended prematurely with myself and another taking three areas (5 player game)... Or at least it felt premature.  Of the numerous house rules, many people pick +1 city for every member of the alliance, but I can see where this would extend the game dramatically.  Having to get 3 was hard enough, and occurred because of a lucky low-odds attack.  Getting 4 would have been impossible.  Limiting alliances to 2 players?  Probably not necessary since good groups will not do lame alliance stuff anyway.  Hmmm.  I probably need to play this more....

RATING: N/A
Purchased as a high school gamer - and avid fan of the books - in 1982 or 1983.*

[i]Dune[/i] holds the record for the game that I have owned the longest without playing. A few weeks after bringing it home, I set it up solo and pushed some pieces around - but that was as far as I got.

I love 2-player wargames, even long ones, but I've since learned to avoid long multiplayer wargames.  There's too much diplomacy and downtime for my tastes, which is why 2-player games are so appealing.

So [i]Dune[/i] is one skeleton that may sit in my closet forever.


*I haven't cracked one of the novels in 25 years, so I have no idea if they still hold up.

RATING: 10
[b][i]A beginning is a very delicate time. Know then, that is is the year 10191. The known universe is ruled by the Padishah Emperor Shaddam the Fourth, my father. In this time, the most precious substance in the universe is the spice Melange. The spice extends life. The spice expands consciousness. The spice is vital to space travel. The Spacing Guild and its navigators, who the spice has mutated over 4000 years, use the orange spice gas, which gives them the ability to fold space. That is, travel to any part of the universe without moving. Oh, yes. I forgot to tell you. The spice exists on only one planet in the entire universe. A desolate, dry planet with vast deserts. Hidden away within the rocks of these deserts are a people known as the Fremen, who have long held a prophecy that a man would come, a messiah, who would lead them to true freedom. The planet is Arrakis, also known as Dune. [/b][/i]


[i]I own the Shai-Hulud box art edition, for those that give a damn about these things.[/i]

We've only ever played with six players, so I'm not sure how well the game scales.

[i]You will need to house rule this game![/i]

[b]Recommendations:[/b]

A. Only play using Scott Everts' fantastic (Artscow printed) redesigned decks and map.

B. No expansions.

C. No advanced or optional rules from the [i]General[/i].

D. Do not allow more than 2 players to form an alliance together.

E. Change the victory conditions:
To win you need N+2 strongholds (whereby N is the number of players in an alliance).


I should plead for CHOAM charity: this game cost me a small fortune to assemble, even AFTER winning the game in an eBay auction for no small sum. So here's the inventory of extras I assembled for the ultimate Dune experience:
1. The actual game = £45
2. Replacement, custom made Artscow decks for the Treachery, Spice, Bene Gesserit, and Traitor decks = £30
3. Replacement, custom made, clearer and larger, Dune map from China (Artscow again) = £20
4. Mounting said map on a large, tough board = £23
5. Printing off a decent player aid = £0
6. Buying 10 packets of plastic discs to replace the card tokens = £20
7. Spray painting 2 lots of discs to colour match the leaders (spray cans cost you know!) = £5
8. Buying a big bag of blue gems to represent the Spice Melange = £10

Total = £153!

All that's left, is to buy or assemble a life size sandworm, and fit all my players with blue contacts, methinks.

[b]And how can this be? For he IS the Kwisatz Haderach![/b]

RATING: 5
Strange game that strange people like. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A thematically rich game made even better if you are a fan of the Dune universe.  I really like the special powers for each of the six factions, as well as the benefits they confer to their allies.  Not very impressed by the standard win condition though, except for the Bene Gesserit.  I was more intrigued by the optional victories.

I also didn't care for the fact that some factions have easier access to spices than others.  There is no worse feeling than having very little army presence on board while being broke at the same time.  The game become quite boring and no one would really want to ally with you.  Not a fan of the combat either.  The whole thing with the committing tokens to using weapon/shield to betrayal just seemed very stilted.

I'm glad to have tried it, but it's definitely not the kind of games I enjoy playing.
